        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c14334"><meta property="og:title" content="Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/acsami.1c14334.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="High-surface-area carbon (HSAC) has been regarded as one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, it generally suffers from low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which is closely related to the formation process of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Herein, the impact of different electrolyte salts on the electrochemical performance and SEI formation of a commercial HSAC anode is studied. It is found that the use of NaCF3SO3 enables much higher ICE (69.28%) and reversible capacity (283 mA h g–1) of the HSAC anode compared with the NaPF6 electrolyte (59.65%, 243 mA h g–1). Through comprehensive characterizations, the improvement in electrochemical performance facilitated by NaCF3SO3 could be attributed to the reduced amount of NaxC and the thinner SEI formed on the surface of HSAC during the initial cycle, which not only provides extra active sites for Na+ storage but also contributes to the promoted ICE. This work not only provides a deeper understanding of the role of electrolyte salt in SEI formation in the HSAC anode but also proposes a new method to further promote the ICE of the HSAC anode in sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/acsami.1c14334.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da88144e76b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da88144e76b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'cfe5e1e5-2299-41c5-b778-427e54c3f9c9'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da88144e76b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90da88144e76b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Impact of Electrolyte Salts on Na Storage Performance for High-Surface-Area Carbon Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c14334", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="loa-info-affiliations-info">School of Advanced Materials, Shenzhen Graduate School, Peking University, Shenzhen 518055, People’s Republic of China</div></div><span class="conrtib-corresp"><strong>*</strong>Email: <a href="mailto:panfeng@pkusz.edu.cn">panfeng@pkusz.edu.cn</a></span><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Feng++Pan" data-id="article_author_search">Feng Pan</a></span></div><div class="loa-info-orcid"><img height="20" width="27" link">https://orcid.org/0000-0002-8216-1339</a></div></div></div></span></li></ul><div class="article_header-footer"><div class="article_header-footer-left"><div class="article_header-links"><input issue&nbsp;</span><time datetime="2021-10-20" class="article__history__time">20 October 2021</time></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="hidden" property="articleSection">research-article</div><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D48745%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYiwei%2BLi%252C%2BShiming%2BChen%252C%2BShenyang%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D41%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c14334%26title%3DImpact%2Bof%2BElectrolyte%2BSalts%2Bon%2BNa%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bfor%2BHigh-Surface-Area%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D48752%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2021" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">High-surface-area carbon (HSAC) has been regarded as one of the most promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, it generally suffers from low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which is closely related to the formation process of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI). Herein, the impact of different electrolyte salts on the electrochemical performance and SEI formation of a commercial HSAC anode is studied. It is found that the use of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> enables much higher ICE (69.28%) and reversible capacity (283 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) of the HSAC anode compared with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte (59.65%, 243 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Through comprehensive characterizations, the improvement in electrochemical performance facilitated by NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> could be attributed to the reduced amount of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C and the thinner SEI formed on the surface of HSAC during the initial cycle, which not only provides extra active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage but also contributes to the promoted ICE. This work not only provides a deeper understanding of the role of electrolyte salt in SEI formation in the HSAC anode but also proposes a new method to further promote the ICE of the HSAC anode in sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D48745%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYiwei%2BLi%252C%2BShiming%2BChen%252C%2BShenyang%2BXu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D41%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c14334%26title%3DImpact%2Bof%2BElectrolyte%2BSalts%2Bon%2BNa%2BStorage%2BPerformance%2Bfor%2BHigh-Surface-Area%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D48752%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Surface%20chemistry" class="article__tags__link">Surface chemistry</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291354&amp;ref=fulltext" title="X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_X-ray%20photoelectron%20spectroscopy" class="article__tags__link">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrolyte&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrolyte">electrolyte</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Solid+Electrolyte+Interface&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_solid%20electrolyte%20interface">solid electrolyte interface</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=High-surface-area+Carbon+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_high-surface-area%20carbon%20anode">high-surface-area carbon anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Initial+Coulombic+Efficiency&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_initial%20Coulombic%20efficiency">initial Coulombic efficiency</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Energy storage has become one of the most important issues in recent years, and different kinds of energy storage systems have gradually sprung up. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), owing to their high energy density, environmental friendliness, and high safety, have been widely utilized in electronic devices and electric vehicles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2−4)</a> Concerned by the increasing consumption rate of lithium resources, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> researchers have considered sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as a promising candidate to substitute LIBs in the future due to their low cost. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6−9)</a> Carbon-based materials, including hard carbon and high-surface-area carbon (HSAC), have been widely reported owing to their low price, high stability, light weight, and high energy density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 10 to 14">(10−14)</a> Compared with hard carbon, the HSAC anode with a porous structure displayed better rate performance and cycling stability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15 to 18">(15−18)</a> However, it still suffers from low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), which severely restricts its practical applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The value of ICE for carbon-based anodes is determined by the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) formation process, which could be manipulated by tuning the electrolyte components. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20−23)</a> In the previous reports, an electrolyte with ether solvent was beneficial to carbon anodes by promoting their electrochemical performance, including graphite <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> and hard carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> In recent years, it has been reported that the ICE and reversible capacity of the HSAC anode can be greatly boosted by choosing ether-based solvents over ester-based ones. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26,27)</a> It has also been reported that the better reversibility and cyclability of the hard carbon anode could be achieved in NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte compared with NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte (ester solvent). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> In addition, Passerini et al. found that the organic and inorganic components in the SEI of the hard carbon anode was affected by the Na salts in the electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> However, few reports have focused on the impact of Na salts on the electrochemical performance and SEI for HSAC anodes until now.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we comprehensively analyzed the impact of Na salts on the electrochemical performance of a commercial HSAC anode in an ether electrolyte by comparing two different Na salts: sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) salt and sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>, which could be also written as NaOTf). The ICE and reversible capacity have been obviously promoted in the NaOTf electrolyte when compared with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Combined with electrochemical impedance, transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results, we discovered that in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, large amounts of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, an electrochemically inert compound, was formed on the HSAC anode during the initial cycle. By contrast, a thinner SEI, less Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, and more reversible Na<sup>+</sup> can be achieved in HSAC anodes when cycled in the NaOTf electrolyte, resulting in a large ICE and higher reversible capacity.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Preparation of HSAC and Hard Carbon</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The HSAC (XFP05, XFNANO, China) and hard carbon (Guangdong Canrd New Energy Technology Co., Ltd., China) were directly utilized without any further treatment.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Characterization Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction with Cu Kα (XRD, Bruker D8-Advance diffractometer, λ = 1.5405 Å) was utilized to test the structure of HSAC. The morphology of the HSAC anode before and after cycling was determined by a scanning electron microscope (SEM, ZEISS Supra 55 field-emission scanning electron microscopy) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM, FEI TecnaiG2 F30). The corresponding element content was analyzed by an attached energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS). The detailed structure of the HSAC at the atomic scale was performed by a high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM). The specific surface areas of HSAC and hard carbon were tested by an adsorption and specific surface area tester (Micromeritics ASAP 2020M+C) by a nitrogen adsorption/desorption method, and the pore size distribution was analyzed by the adsorption isotherm according to the Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) model. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, ESCALAB 250XL) measurements were used to analyze the element distribution on the surface of the HSAC anode before and after cycling. The electrodes were washed with dimethyl carbonate (DMC) solvent to remove the contaminants on the surface and then dried in the glovebox for XPS measurements. All of the samples have been transferred in vacuum and etched by Ar<sup>+</sup> ions for 60 s before testing. During XPS measurements, the base pressure of the sample chamber was kept below 3.0 × 10<sup>–10</sup> mbar.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Testing</h3><div class="NLM_p last">HSAC, acetylene black, and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) were poured into <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent with a weight ratio of 7:2:1. The mixture was stirred for 10 h to obtain a uniform slurry. Then, the slurry was spread on Cu foil and dried at 120 °C in a vacuum oven. Coin cells (CR2032) were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with water and oxygen below 1 ppm. Pure sodium and glass fiber (Whatman GF/A) were utilized as the counter electrode and separator, respectively. The electrode was cycled in the electrolyte of 1 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether and 1 M sodium trifluoromethanesulfonate (NaOTf) in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether, respectively. The as-prepared cells were equally discharged and charged between 0.01 and 3.0 V by a NEWARE system. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance tests have been collected by an electrochemistry workstation (1400 cell test system, Solartron).</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The detailed information of as-prepared HSAC is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1 and S2</a>. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>, a wide (002) peak could be observed at around 21.4°, corresponding to disordered carbon layers in HSAC with an average layer distance of ∼4.16 Å. The XPS spectra of C 1s (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>) can be divided into four peaks that correspond to C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The SEM image of HSAC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1c</a>) exhibits a porous structure, consisting of mesopores and macropores, as shown in the TEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1d</a>), which is beneficial to Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The HRTEM image and the corresponding fast Fourier transform (FFT) of HSAC (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1e</a>) exhibit an average distance between carbon layers of 4.292 Å as well as no distinct diffraction patterns, which is in good agreement with the XRD result in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>. The specific surface area and the distribution of pore size of HSAC are also measured (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). On the one hand, HSAC exhibits a specific surface area of 293.85 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, which enables Na<sup>+</sup> storage on the surface (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>); <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> on the other hand, HSAC mainly contains macropores (100 nm) and a small amount of meso/micropores, which is beneficial to the fast diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. During the initial cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a), NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes delivered similar initial discharge capacities (about 565 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). In addition, the capacity–voltage profile of the HSAC anode is slopping in the whole voltage range, implying that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage process is capacitive-dominated. Nevertheless, the initial charging capacity in the NaOTf electrolyte is higher than that in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, corresponding to an improved ICE (from 59.65 to 69.28%). The promoted ICE for the HSAC anode in the NaOTf electrolyte is higher than most of the previously reported HSAC anodes, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>. More importantly, the extra capacity in the NaOTf electrolyte is retained at the second cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). A similar phenomenon could be discovered in the normalized CV results, (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d), where the absence of sharp redox peaks implies the capacitive-controlled behavior and the extra charge (colored area) obtained in the NaOTf electrolyte is in line with the extra capacity. It should be noted that a broad reduction peak at around 0.7 V can be observed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c), which is attributed to the irreversible SEI formation. The intensity of this peak for the NaOTf electrolyte is lower than that for the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, suggesting less electrolyte is consumed to form SEI in the NaOTf electrolyte, which is beneficial to a higher ICE. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e shows the cycling performance of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes at a higher specific current (200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, after 3 cycles activation at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The HSAC anode in the NaOTf electrolyte exhibits a higher initial capacity  (283 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) than that in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. During long-term cycling, capacity decay could be barely observed in both cells after 100 cycles (capacity–voltage profiles are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), demonstrating the superior cycling performance of the HSAC anode as well as the high reversibility of the extra capacity (∼40 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). Meanwhile, the rate performance of the HSAC anode cycled in different electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 1</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a>) shows that the extra capacity is not affected by charge/discharge rates, inferring fast reaction kinetics.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) First and (b) second capacity–voltage profiles of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (the extra capacity between NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes is marked by pink dashed arrows). (c) First and (d) second cycles of CV curves for the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> (the peaks related to SEI are marked by blue dashed circle, and the extra capacity of the HSAC anode between NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes is marked by pink shadow). (e) Cycling performance with a current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (after 3 cycle activation at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and (f) rate performance of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes (about 40 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> discrepancy of the HSAC anode between NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes is marked by pink dashed arrows).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As mentioned above, higher ICE and reversible capacity have been achieved for the HSAC anode with the help of NaOTf. To further explore whether the promoted electrochemical performance in the NaOTf electrolyte is characteristic for HSAC, commercial hard carbon has been chosen as the comparison group. Interestingly, no distinct difference could be observed in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). Considering that hard carbon possesses a much lower specific surface area (2.79 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) than HSAC (see detailed data in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), the promoted electrochemical performance observed in the HSAC anode might be attributed to the unique Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior originated from its porous structure and high specific surface area.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes, the normalized CV curves with different scan rates are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b, respectively. The charge storage of diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled processes could be analyzed by<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) represents the corresponding current at the specific voltage and ν represents the corresponding scan rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> Thus, the a and b values could be obtained by the intercept and slope of the log (<i>i</i>) versus log (ν) plot. When the b value is close to 0.5, the process is close to diffusion-controlled and when the <i>b</i> value is close to 1, the process is close to capacitive-controlled. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Four different conditions (D1, D2, C1, and C2) have been selected to compare the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes, and the corresponding fitting curves with the calculated slope are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d. The <i>b</i> values for different conditions range between 0.851 and 0.995, suggesting the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behaviors for HSAC in different electrolytes are mainly capacitive-controlled. Additionally, according to the equation<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>the diffusion-controlled and capacitive-controlled capacity could also be quantitatively calculated. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a><i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>1/2</sup> correspond to the capacitive-controlled and diffusion-controlled contributions to the total current, respectively. The specific contribution at the specific potential could be quantified through confirming <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e shows that under a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the capacitive contributions in NaOTf and NaPF<sub>6</sub> are 71 and 69%, respectively. In addition, the capacitive contributions will gradually increase with the scan rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f), which agrees with the previous results. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> From the results above, it could be concluded that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior for the HSAC anode is mainly capacitive-controlled, regardless of the choice of Na salts. To further prove that the Na<sup>+</sup>-ion storage behavior is capacitive-controlled, HRTEM images of the HSAC electrode before and after the initial discharge and charge are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. The layer distance of the HSAC anode barely changed after the initial discharge and charge in both NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes, further demonstrating that the electrochemical behavior for the HSAC anode is capacitive-controlled. Therefore, the electrochemical behavior of HSAC is dominated by the surface adsorption and pore-filling processes, which are closely associated with the formation of SEI.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a)–(b) Normalized CV curves at different scan rates (0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) of the HSAC anode in (a) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (b) NaOTf electrolytes. (c)–(d) Fitted linear relationship between log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>ν</i>) and the calculated <i>b</i> values at the selected voltage conditions (marked by green dashed lines in (a) and (b)) in (c) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (d) NaOTf electrolytes. (e) Capacitive contribution in the CV curves of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf (inset on the top right corner) at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (f) Capacitive contribution ratio of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf at different scan rates (0.2, 1, and 5 mV s<sup>–1</sup>).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The SEI of the HSAC anode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes is then analyzed by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy and <i>ex situ</i> TEM. The impedance spectra of HSAC anodes after the initial discharge/charge process are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a,b. According to our fitting results (the corresponding equivalent circuit is shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a), both interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) and charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) for the HSAC anode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte are larger than those in NaOTf after the initial discharge and charge process (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), implying that a thicker SEI formed in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. This could be confirmed by the TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c,d) of HSAC electrodes after the initial discharge process (the TEM images of the HSAC electrode before cycling are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). The distinct porous structure disappeared after the initial discharge  in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte owing to the formation of SEI. However, part of the nanopores could still be retained after the initial discharge  in the NaOTf electrolyte, implying less SEI has formed. TEM images with the corresponding EDS mapping images of Na, F, and O elements in the HSAC anode after the initial charge in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes are also shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f (the corresponding EDS mapping images of P and S are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), and the specific element ratio is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>. The contents of Na, F, and O detected on HSAC cycled in NaOTf are less than those in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, inferring a thinner SEI. Similar results can be observed in SEM images and the corresponding element distribution of HSAC anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10 and Table S4</a>). Therefore, the improved ICE for the HSAC anode in NaOTf electrolyte could be ascribed to the thinner SEI formed on the surface.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a)–(b) Electrochemical impedance spectra of the HSAC electrode after the initial (a) discharge and (b) charge process in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes (from 0.01 to 100 000 Hz). The corresponding enlarged semicircles are shown in the insets. The circuit model for fitting the impedance semicircles is shown in the inset of (a). (c)–(d) TEM images of the HSAC anode after the initial discharge process in (c) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (d) NaOTf electrolytes. The nanopores in (d) are marked by red arrows. (e)–(f) TEM image and the corresponding element distribution of Na, F, and O in the HSAC anode after the initial charge in (e) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (f) NaOTf electrolytes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further analyze the chemical composition of SEI on the HSAC electrode, <i>ex situ</i> XPS measurements have been performed. The XPS spectra of the HSAC electrode after the initial discharge and charge are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>, where the ratios of C:O, C:Na, and C:F for the HSAC electrode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte are higher than those in the NaOTf electrolyte, further demonstrating the thinner SEI formed in the NaOTf electrolyte. The XPS spectra of the C 1s signal for HSAC electrodes before cycling (immersed in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes, respectively) and after the initial cycle in different electrolytes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b; each signal has been etched for 60 s by Ar<sup>+</sup> to remove the contaminants on the surface. Each peak can be divided into five peaks located at 283.5, 284.6, 285.2, 286.2, and 288 eV, corresponding to the Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O bonds, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref35 ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 29 and 35 to 37">(29,35−37)</a> The intensity of the C–C peak decreased, and other peaks (e.g., C–O and C═O) related to SEI components increased after the initial cycle, implying the formation of SEI after the initial cycle. Compared with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, the stronger intensity of the C–C bond for the HSAC electrode cycled in the NaOTf electrolyte could be observed, implying that less SEI component formed on the surface, which means thinner SEI on the surface of the HSAC anode in the NaOTf electrolyte. To further explore the variation on the surface of the HSAC electrode after the initial charge, the corresponding C 1s spectra in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes without etching are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>. Interestingly, a small amount of sodiated HSAC (Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C) could be discovered after the initial cycle in the NaOTf electrolyte, which could not be observed in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Considering that Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C is formed underneath SEI, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> it is inferred that a thinner SEI is formed on the surface of the HSAC anode in the NaOTf electrolyte, which is consistent with the previous results.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a)–(b) XPS spectra of the C 1s signal for the HSAC electrode immersed (pristine) and after the initial cycle (charged to 3 V) in (a) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (b) NaOTf electrolytes. The Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O peaks are marked by orange, pink, green, blue, and purple arrows, respectively. (c)–(d) Specific ratios of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O bonds calculated by the fitted peak area in (a) and (b): (c) immersed in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes and (d) after the initial cycle. (e)–(f) XPS spectra of the O 1s signal for the HSAC electrode immersed (pristine) and after the initial cycle in (e) NaPF<sub>6</sub> and (f) NaOTf electrolytes. The C═O, C–O, and Na KLL peaks are marked by orange, pink, and blue arrows, respectively. (g)–(h) Specific ratios of C═O, C–O, and Na KLL bonds calculated by the fitted peak area in (e) and (f): (g) immersed in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes and (h) after the initial cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">For quantitative analysis, the ratios of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C, C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O bonds after etching are calculated by their corresponding peak areas (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c,d). The ratios of C–C, C–H, C–O, and C═O are similar for the HSAC electrodes immersed in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). However, after the initial cycle, a larger amount of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C could be measured on the surface of the HSAC electrode cycled in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte, which is much severe than in the NaOTf electrolyte (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). Considering that Na ions in Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C are electrochemically inert (the Na ions could still be observed after full charging, meaning that the Na ions in Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C are inert), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> part of the inserted Na<sup>+</sup> during discharging could not be extracted from the HSAC electrode, leading to a low ICE. In addition, the large number of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C on the surface of the HSAC anode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte will contribute to a large amount of invalid Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites, which causes the low reversible capacity in the following cycles. Therefore, the HSAC anode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte exhibits less ICE and reversible capacity compared to those in the NaOTf electrolyte. Moreover, from the O 1s spectra for HSAC electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>h), it could be observed that the C–O:C═O ratio decreased after the initial cycle in both electrolytes. Since C–O could be originated from the residue electrolyte or polyether SEI component while C═O is attributed to the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>R/Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>2</sub>R compounds, the different specific contents of C–O and C═O for the HSAC electrode cycled in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes suggest that the composition of organic SEI varies in the two electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g,h). It should be noted that as another common SEI constituent, NaF in both SEI layers is similar (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/suppl_file/am1c14334_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the above results and discussions, different SEI formation behaviors of the HSAC anode cycled in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes are schematically illustrated in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>. A thick SEI containing NaF, C═O compounds (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>R), and polyether formed on the surface of the HSAC electrode in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. Besides, a large amount of electrochemically inert compound Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C formed on the surface, which causes the consumption of sodium ions, together with the formation of SEI, contributing to a low ICE in the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. By contrast, fewer C═O compounds (Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>2</sub>R/Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>2</sub>R) and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C are formed on the surface of the HSAC anode in the NaOTf electrolyte, which contributes to a thinner SEI and more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, effectively promoting the ICE and reversible capacity of the HSAC electrode during long-term cycles.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/medium/am1c14334_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0006.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Illustration of the Different SEI Formed on the Surface of the HSAC Electrode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf Electrolytes and the Corresponding Mechanism of the Promoted ICE and Reversible Capacity in the NaOTf Electrolyte</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c14334/asset/images/large/am1c14334_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c14334&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The different electrochemical performances of the commercial HSAC electrode in NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaOTf electrolytes have been systematically researched. It is found that the use of NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> enables much higher ICE and reversible capacity of HSAC compared with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte. The promoted electrochemical performance in the NaOTf electrolyte can be explained by the different surface behaviors: a thinner SEI and smaller amount of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>C formed on the surface of HSAC in NaOTf, which jointly contributes to less Na consumption during the initial cycle as well as facilitates more electrochemically active sites, causing higher ICE and more reversible capacity during long-term cycling. By revealing the correlation between the electrolyte and the electrochemical performance, this work provides useful insights into the optimization of the electrolyte/electrode interface and the promotion of electrochemical performance in carbon-based anodes for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i                         cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A brief review of catalytic cathode materials for Na-CO2 batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sui, Dong; Chang, Meijia; Wang, Haiyu; Qian, Hang; Yang, Yanliang; Li, Shan; Zhang, Yongsheng; Song, Yingze</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Catalysts</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">603</span>CODEN:
